10138-8{_}531{_}But Don John succeeded in obtaining their liberation.
10138-8{_}532{_}Unfortunately, the elder died--of a broken heart, it is said--at Naples.
10138-8{_}533{_}The younger was sent home, with three of his attendants, for whom he had an especial regard.
10138-8{_}534{_}Don John declined the present, which he gave to Fatima's brother.
10138-8{_}535{_}In a letter to the Turkish princess, he remarked, that "he had done this, not because he undervalued her beautiful gift, but because it had ever been the habit of his royal ancestors freely to grant favors to those who stood in need of their protection, but not to receive aught by way of recompense."
10138-8{_}536{_}THE WIND AND STREAM.
10138-8{_}537{_}A brook came stealing from the ground;       You scarcely saw its silvery gleam     Among the herbs that hung around       The borders of that winding stream,--       A pretty stream, a placid stream,       A softly gliding, bashful stream.
10138-8{_}538{_}A breeze came wandering from the sky,       Light as the whispers of a dream;     He put the o'erhanging grasses by,       And gayly stooped to kiss the stream,--       The pretty stream, the flattered stream,       The shy, yet unreluctant stream.
10138-8{_}539{_}The water, as the wind passed o'er,       Shot upward many a glancing beam,     Dimpled and quivered more and more,       And tripped along a livelier stream,--       The flattered stream, the simpering stream,       The fond, delighted, silly stream.
10138-8{_}540{_}Away the airy wanderer flew       To where the fields with blossoms teem,     To sparkling springs and rivers blue,       And left alone that little stream,--       The flattered stream, the cheated stream,       The sad, forsaken, lonely stream.
10138-8{_}541{_}That careless wind no more came back;       He wanders yet the fields, I deem;     But on its melancholy track       Complaining went that little stream,--       The cheated stream, the hopeless stream,       The ever murmuring, moaning stream.
10138-8{_}542{_}TURKEY TRACKS.
10138-8{_}543{_}Don't open your eyes, Polder!
10138-8{_}544{_}You think I am going to tell you about some of my Minnesota experiences; how I used to scamper over the prairies on my Indian pony, and lie in wait for wild turkeys on the edge of an oak opening.
10138-8{_}545{_}That is pretty sport, too, to creep under an oak with low-hanging boughs, and in the silence of a glowing autumn-day linger by the hour together in a trance of warm stillness, watching the light tracery of shadow and sun on that smooth sward, only now and then roused by the fleet rush of a deer through the wood, or the brisk chatter of a plume-tailed squirrel, till one hears a distant, sharp, clucking chuckle, and in an instant more pulls the trigger, and upsets a grand old cock, every bronzed feather glittering in the sunshine, and now splashed with scarlet blood, the delicate underwing ground into down as he rolls and flutters; for the first shot rarely kills at once with an amateur; there's too much excitement.
10138-8{_}546{_}Splendid sport, that!
10138-8{_}547{_}but I'm not going into it second-hand.
10138-8{_}548{_}I promised to tell you a story, now the skipper's fast, and the night is too warm to think of sleep down in that wretched bunk;--what another torture Dante might have lavished on his Inferno, if he'd ever slept in a fishing-smack!
10138-8{_}549{_}No.
10138-8{_}550{_}The moonlight makes me sentimental!
10138-8{_}551{_}Did I ever tell you about a month I spent up in Centreville, the year I came home from Germany?
10138-8{_}552{_}That was turkey-hunting with a vengeance!
10138-8{_}553{_}You see, my pretty cousin Peggy married Peter Smith, who owns paper-mills in Centreville, and has exiled herself into deep country for life; a circumstance I disapprove, because I like Peggy, and manufacturers always bore me, though Peter is a clever fellow enough; but madam was an old flame of mine, and I have a lingering tenderness for her yet.
10138-8{_}554{_}I wish she was nearer town.
10138-8{_}555{_}Just that year Peggy had been very ill indeed, and Kate, her sister, had gone up to nurse her.
10138-8{_}556{_}When I came home Peggy was getting better, and sent for me to come up and make a visitation there in June.
10138-8{_}557{_}I hadn't seen Kate for seven years,--not since she was thirteen; our education intervened.

10138-8{_}574{_}Such a pretty walk it was, too!
10138-8{_}575{_}through a thicket of birches, down a little hill-side into a hollow full of hoary chestnut-trees, across a bubbling, dancing brook, and you came out upon the tiniest orchard in the world, a one-storied house with a red porch, and a great sweet-brier bush thereby; while up the hill-side behind stretched a high picket fence, enclosing huge trees, part of the same brook I had crossed here dammed into a pond, and a chicken-house of pretentious height and aspect,--one of those model institutions that are the ruin of gentlemen-farmers and the delight of women.
10138-8{_}576{_}I had to go into the farm-kitchen for the poultry-yard key.
10138-8{_}577{_}The door stood open, and I stepped in cautiously, lest I should come unaware upon some domestic scene not intended to be visible to the naked eye.
10138-8{_}578{_}And a scene I did come upon, fit for Retzsch to outline;--the cleanest kitchen, a dresser of white wood under one window, and the farmer's daughter, Melinda Tucker, moulding bread thereat in a ponderous tray; her deep red hair,--yes, it was red and comely!
10138-8{_}579{_}of the deepest bay, full of gilded reflections, and accompanied by the fair, rose-flushed skin, blue eyes, and scarlet lips that belong to such hair,--which, as I began to say, was puckered into a thousand curves trying to curl, and knotted strictly against a pretty head, while her calico frock-sleeves were pinned-back to the shoulders, baring such a dimpled pair of arms,--how they did fly up and down in the tray!
10138-8{_}580{_}I stood still contemplating the picture, and presently seeing her begin to strip the dough from her pink fingers and mould it into a mass, I ventured to knock.

10138-8{_}612{_}(Hannah is my sister.)
10138-8{_}613{_}I could only see two little turkeys,--both on the floor of the second-story parlor in the chicken-house, both flat on their backs and gasping.

10138-8{_}644{_}Melindy was a very pretty girl, and it was very good fun to see her blue eyes open and her red lips laugh over my European experiences.
10138-8{_}645{_}Really, I began to be of some importance at the farm-house, and to take airs upon myself, I suppose; but I was not conscious of the fact at the time.
10138-8{_}646{_}After a week or two, Melindy and I began to have bad luck with the turkeys.
10138-8{_}647{_}I found two drenched and shivering, after a hail-and-thunder storm, and setting them in a basket on the cooking-stove hearth, went to help Melindy "dress her bow-pot," as she called arranging a vase of flowers, and when I came back the little turkeys were singed; they died a few hours after.
10138-8{_}648{_}Two more were trodden on by a great Shanghai rooster, who was so tall he could not see where he set his feet down; and of the remaining pair, one disappeared mysteriously,--supposed to be rats; and one falling into the duck-pond, Melindy began to dry it in her apron, and I went to help her; I thought, as I was rubbing the thing down with the apron, while she held it, that I had found one of her soft dimpled hands, and I gave the luckless turkey such a tender pressure that it uttered a miserable squeak and departed this life.
10138-8{_}649{_}Melindy all but cried.
10138-8{_}650{_}I laughed irresistibly.
10138-8{_}651{_}So there were no more turkeys.
10138-8{_}652{_}Peggy began to wonder what they should do for the proper Thanksgiving dinner, and Peter turned restlessly on his sofa, quite convinced that everything was going to rack and ruin because he had a sprained ankle.
10138-8{_}653{_}"Can't we buy some young turkeys?"

10138-8{_}664{_}I was going right down to the farm-house to console Melindy, and take her a book she wanted to read, for no fine lady of all my New York acquaintance enjoyed a good book more than she did; but Cousin Kate asked me to wind some yarn for her, and was so brilliant, so amiable, so altogether charming, I quite forgot Melindy till dinner-time, and then, when that was over, there was a basket to be found, and we were off,--turkey-hunting!
10138-8{_}665{_}Down hill-sides overhung with tasselled chestnut-boughs; through pine-woods where neither horse nor wagon intruded any noise of hoof or wheel upon the odorous silence, as we rolled over the sand, past green meadows, and sloping orchards; over little bright brooks that chattered musically to the bobolinks on the fence-posts, and were echoed by those sacerdotal gentlemen in such liquid, bubbling, rollicking, uproarious bursts of singing as made one think of Anacreon's grasshopper       "Drunk with morning's dewy wine."

10138-8{_}684{_}I wanted to see Kate's enjoyment of a scene that Kensett or Church should have made immortal long ago:--a wide stretch of hill and valley, quivering with cornfields, rolled away in pasture lands, thick with sturdy woods, or dotted over with old apple-trees, whose dense leaves caught the slant sunshine, glowing on their tops, and deepening to a dark, velvety green below, and far, far away, on the broad blue sky, the lurid splendors of a thunder-cloud, capped with pearly summits, tower upon tower, sharply defined against the pure ether, while in its purple base forked lightnings sped to and fro, and revealed depths of waiting tempest that could not yet descend.

10138-8{_}768{_}I meant to have gone down to the farm-house after tea, and inquired about the safety of my prizes, but Kate wanted to play chess.
10138-8{_}769{_}Peter couldn't, and Peggy wouldn't; I had to, of course, and we played late.
10138-8{_}770{_}Kate had such pretty hands; long taper fingers, rounded to the tiniest rosy points; no dimples, but full muscles, firm and exquisitely moulded; and the dainty way in which she handled her men was half the game to me;--I lost it; I played wretchedly.
10138-8{_}771{_}The next day Kate went with me to see the turkeys; so she did the day after.
10138-8{_}772{_}We were forgetting Melindy, I am afraid, for it was a week before I remembered I had promised her a new magazine.
10138-8{_}773{_}I recollected myself; then, with a sort of shame, rolled up the number, and went off to the farm-house.
10138-8{_}774{_}It seems Kate was there, busy in the garret, unpacking a bureau that had been stored there, with some of Peggy's foreign purchases, for summer wear, in the drawers.
10138-8{_}775{_}I did not know that.
10138-8{_}776{_}I found Melindy spreading yeast-cakes to dry on a table, just by the north end of the house; a hop-vine in full blossom made a sort of porch-roof over the window by which she stood.
10138-8{_}777{_}"I've brought your book, Melindy," said I.
10138-8{_}778{_}"Thank you, sir," returned she, crisply.
10138-8{_}779{_}"How pretty you look to-day."
10138-8{_}780{_}condescendingly remarked I.
10138-8{_}781{_}"I don't thank you for that, sir;--       "'Praise to the face       Is open disgrace!'"
10138-8{_}782{_}was all the response.
10138-8{_}783{_}"Why, Melindy!
10138-8{_}784{_}what makes you so cross?"
10138-8{_}785{_}inquired I, in a tone meant to be tenderly reproachful,--in the mean time attempting to possess myself of her hand; for, to be honest, Polder, I had been a little sweet to the girl before Kate drove her out of my head.
10138-8{_}786{_}The hand was snatched away.
10138-8{_}787{_}I tried indifference.
10138-8{_}788{_}"How are the turkeys to-day.
10138-8{_}789{_}Melindy?"
10138-8{_}790{_}Here Joe, an _enfant terrible,_ came upon the scene suddenly.
10138-8{_}791{_}"Them turkeys eats a lot, Mister Greene.
10138-8{_}792{_}Melindy says there's one on 'em struts jes' like you, 'n' makes as much gabble."
10138-8{_}793{_}"Gobble!
10138-8{_}794{_}gobble!
10138-8{_}795{_}gobble!"
10138-8{_}796{_}echoed an old turkey from somewhere; I thought it was overhead, but I saw nothing.
10138-8{_}797{_}Melindy threw her apron over her face and laughed till her arms grew red.
10138-8{_}798{_}I picked up my hat and walked off.
10138-8{_}799{_}For three days I kept out of that part of the Smith demesne, I assure you!
10138-8{_}800{_}Kate began to grow mocking and derisive; she teased me from morning till night, and the more she teased me, the more I adored her.
10138-8{_}801{_}I was getting desperate, when one Sunday night Kate asked me to walk down to the farm-house with her after tea, as Mrs. Tucker was sick, and she had something to take to her.
10138-8{_}802{_}We found the old woman sitting up in the kitchen, and as full of talk as ever, though an unlucky rheumatism kept her otherwise quiet.
10138-8{_}803{_}"How do the turkeys come on, Mrs.
10138-8{_}804{_}Tucker?"
10138-8{_}805{_}said I, by way of conversation.
10138-8{_}806{_}"Well, I declare, you han't heerd about them turkeys, hev ye?
10138-8{_}807{_}You see they was doin' fine, and father he went off to salt for a spell, so's to see'f 'twouldn't stop a complaint he's got,--I do'no' but it's a spine in the back,--makes him kinder' faint by spells, so's he loses his conscientiousness all to once; so he left the chickens 'n' things for Melindy to boss, 'n' she got somethin' else into her head, 'n' she left the door open one night, and them ten turkeys they up and run away, I'xpect they took to the woods, 'fore Melindy brought to mind how't she hadn't shut the door.
10138-8{_}808{_}She's set out fur to hunt 'em.
10138-8{_}809{_}I shouldn't wonder'f she was out now, seein' it's arter sundown."
10138-8{_}810{_}"She a'n't nuther!"
10138-8{_}811{_}roared the terrible Joe, from behind the door, where he had retreated at my coming.
10138-8{_}812{_}"She's settin' on a flour-barrel down by the well, an' George Bemont's a-huggin' on her"  Good gracious!
10138-8{_}813{_}what a slap Mrs. Tucker fetched that unlucky child, with a long brown towel that hung at hand!
10138-8{_}814{_}and how he howled!
10138-8{_}815{_}while Kate exploded with laughter, in spite of her struggles to keep quiet.
10138-8{_}816{_}"He _is_ the dre'fullest boy!"
10138-8{_}817{_}whined Mrs. Tucker.
10138-8{_}818{_}"Melindy tells how he sassed you 'tother day, Mr. Greene.
10138-8{_}819{_}I shall hev to tewtor that boy; he's got to hev the rod, I guess!"
10138-8{_}820{_}I bade Mrs. Tucker good night, for Kate was already out of the door, and, before I knew what she was about, had taken a by-path in sight of the well; and there, to be sure, sat Melindy, on a prostrate flour-barrel that was rolled to the foot of the big apple-tree, twirling her fingers in pretty embarrassment, and held on her insecure perch by the stout arm of George Bemont, a handsome brown fellow, evidently very well content just now.
10138-8{_}821{_}"Pretty,--isn't it?"
10138-8{_}822{_}said Kate.
10138-8{_}823{_}"Very,--quite pastoral," sniffed I.
10138-8{_}824{_}We were sitting round the open door an hour after, listening to a whippoorwill, and watching the slow moon rise over a hilly range just east of Centreville, when that elvish little "week!
10138-8{_}825{_}week!"
10138-8{_}826{_}piped out of the wood that lay behind the house.
10138-8{_}827{_}"That is hopeful," said Kate; "I think Melindy and George must have tracked the turkeys to their haunt, and scared them homeward."
10138-8{_}828{_}"George--who?"
10138-8{_}829{_}said Peggy.
10138-8{_}830{_}"George Bemont; it seems he is--what is your Connecticut phrase?--sparkin' Melindy."
10138-8{_}831{_}"I'm very glad; he is a clever fellow," said Peter.
10138-8{_}832{_}"And she is such a very pretty girl," continued Peggy,--"so intelligent and graceful; don't you think so, Sam?"
10138-8{_}833{_}"Aw, yes, well enough for a rustic," said I, languidly.
10138-8{_}834{_}"I never could endure red hair, though!"
10138-8{_}835{_}Kate stopped on the door-sill; she had risen to go up stairs.
10138-8{_}836{_}"Gobble!
10138-8{_}837{_}gobble!
10138-8{_}838{_}gobble!"
10138-8{_}839{_}mocked she.
10138-8{_}840{_}I had heard that once before!
10138-8{_}841{_}Peter and Peggy roared;--they knew it all;--I was sold!
10138-8{_}842{_}"Cure me of Kate Stevens?"
10138-8{_}843{_}Of course it did.
10138-8{_}844{_}I never saw her again without wanting to fight shy, I was so sure of an allusion to turkeys.
10138-8{_}845{_}No, I took the first down train.
10138-8{_}846{_}There are more pretty girls in New York, twice over, than there are in Centreville, I console myself; but, by George!
10138-8{_}847{_}Polder, Kate Stevens was charming!--Look out there!
10138-8{_}848{_}don't meddle with the skipper's coils of rope!
10138-8{_}849{_}can't you sleep on deck without a pillow?
10138-8{_}850{_}ROBIN HOOD.
10138-8{_}851{_}There is no one of the royal heroes of England that enjoys a more enviable reputation than the bold outlaw of Barnsdale and Sherwood.
10138-8{_}852{_}His chance for a substantial immortality is at least as good as that of stout Lion-Heart, wild Prince Hal, or merry Charles.
10138-8{_}853{_}His fame began with the yeomanry full five hundred years ago, was constantly increasing for two or three centuries, has extended to all classes of society, and, with some changes of aspect, is as great as ever.
10138-8{_}854{_}Bishops, sheriffs, and game-keepers, the only enemies he ever had, have relinquished their ancient grudges, and Englishmen would be almost as loath to surrender his exploits as any part of the national glory.
10138-8{_}855{_}His free life in the woods, his unerring eye and strong arm, his open hand and love of fair play, his never forgotten courtesy, his respect for women and devotion to Mary, form a picture eminently healthful and agreeable to the imagination, and commend him to the hearty favor of all genial minds.
10138-8{_}856{_}But securely established as Robin Hood is in popular esteem, his historical position is by no means well ascertained, and his actual existence has been a subject of shrewd doubt and discussion.
10138-8{_}857{_}"A tale of Robin Hood" is an old proverb for the idlest of stories; yet all the materials at our command for making up an opinion on these questions are precisely of this description.
10138-8{_}858{_}They consist, that is to say, of a few ballads of unknown antiquity.
10138-8{_}859{_}These ballads, or others like them, are clearly the authority upon which the statements of the earlier chroniclers who take notice of Robin Hood are founded.
10138-8{_}860{_}They are also, to all appearance, the original source of the numerous and wide-spread traditions concerning him; which, unless the contrary can be shown, must be regarded, according to the almost universal rule in such cases, as having been suggested by the very legends to which, in the vulgar belief, they afford an irresistible confirmation.
10138-8{_}861{_}Various periods, ranging from the time of Richard the First to near the end of the reign of Edward the Second, have been selected by different writers as the age of Robin Hood; but (excepting always the most ancient ballads, which may possibly be placed within these limits) no mention whatever is made of him in literature before the latter half of the reign of Edward the Third.
10138-8{_}862{_}"Rhymes of Robin Hood" are then spoken of by the author of "Piers Ploughman" (assigned to about 1362) as better known to idle fellows than pious songs, and from the manner of the allusion it is a just inference that such rhymes were at that time no novelties.
10138-8{_}863{_}The next notice is in Wyntown's Scottish Chronicle, written about 1420, where the following lines occur--without any connection, and in the form of an entry--under the year 1283:--      "Lytil Jhon and Robyne Hude     Wayth-men ware commendyd gude:     In Yngil-wode and Barnysdale     Thai oysyd all this tyme thare trawale.
10138-8{_}864{_}"[1]   At last we encounter Robin Hood in what may be called history; first of all in a passage of the "Scotichronicon," often quoted, and highly curious as containing the earliest theory upon this subject.
10138-8{_}865{_}The "Scotichronicon" was written partly by Fordun, canon of Aberdeen, between 1377 and 1384, and partly by his pupil Bower, abbot of St. Columba, about 1450.
10138-8{_}866{_}Fordun has the character of a man of judgment and research, and any statement or opinion delivered by him would be entitled to respect.
10138-8{_}867{_}Of Bower not so much can be said.
10138-8{_}868{_}He largely interpolated the work of his master, and sometimes with the absurdest fictions.
10138-8{_}869{_}[2] _Among his interpolations_, and forming, it is important to observe, _no part of the original text_, is a passage translated as follows.
10138-8{_}870{_}It is inserted immediately after Fordun's account of the defeat of Simon de Montfort, and the punishments inflicted on his adherents.
10138-8{_}871{_}"At this time, [_sc_.
10138-8{_}872{_}1266,] from the number of those who had been deprived of their estates arose the celebrated bandit Robert Hood, (with Little John and their accomplices,) whose achievements the foolish vulgar delight to celebrate in comedies and tragedies, while the ballads upon his adventures sung by the jesters and minstrels are preferred to all others.
10138-8{_}873{_}"Some things to his honor are also related, as appears from this.
10138-8{_}874{_}Once on a time, when, having incurred the anger of the king and the prince, he could hear mass nowhere but in Barnsdale, while he was devoutly occupied with the service, (for this was his wont, nor would he ever suffer it to be interrupted for the most pressing occasion,) he was surprised by a certain sheriff and officers of the king, who had often troubled him before, in the secret place in the woods where he was engaged in worship as aforesaid.
10138-8{_}875{_}Some of his men, who had taken the alarm, came to him and begged him to fly with all speed.
10138-8{_}876{_}This, out of reverence for the host, which he was then most devoutly adoring, he positively refused to do.
10138-8{_}877{_}But while the rest of his followers were trembling for their lives, Robert, confiding in Him whom he worshipped, fell on his enemies with a few who chanced to be with him, and easily got the better of them; and having enriched himself with their plunder and ransom, he was led from that time forth to hold ministers of the church and masses in greater veneration than ever, mindful of the common saying, that       "'God hears the man who often hears the mass.'"
10138-8{_}878{_}In another place Bower writes to the same effect: "In this year [1266] the dispossessed barons of England and the royalists were engaged in fierce hostilities.
10138-8{_}879{_}Among the former, Roger Mortimer occupied the Welsh marches, and John Daynil the Isle of Ely.
10138-8{_}880{_}Robert Hood was now living in outlawry among the woodland copses and thickets."
10138-8{_}881{_}Mair, a Scottish writer of the first quarter of the sixteenth century, the next historian who takes cognizance of our hero, and the only other that requires any attention, has a passage which may be considered in connection with the foregoing.
10138-8{_}882{_}In his "Historia Majoris Britanniae" he remarks, under the reign of Richard the First: "About this time [1189-99], as I conjecture, the notorious robbers, Robert Hood of England and Little John, lurked in the woods, spoiling the goods only of rich men.
10138-8{_}883{_}They slew nobody but those who attacked them, or offered resistance in defence of their property.
10138-8{_}884{_}Robert maintained by his plunder a hundred archers, so skilful in fight that four hundred brave men feared to attack them.
10138-8{_}885{_}He suffered no woman to be maltreated, and never robbed the poor, but assisted them abundantly with the wealth which he took from abbots."
10138-8{_}886{_}It appears, then, that contemporaneous history is absolutely silent concerning Robin Hood; that, excepting the casual allusion in "Piers Ploughman," he is first mentioned by a rhyming chronicler who wrote one hundred years after the latest date at which he can possibly be supposed to have lived, and then by two prose chroniclers who wrote about one hundred and twenty-five years and two hundred years respectively after that date; and it is further manifest that all three of these chroniclers had no other authority for their statements than traditional tales similar to those which have come down to our day.
10138-8{_}887{_}When, therefore, Thierry, relying upon these chronicles and kindred popular legends, unhesitatingly adopts the conjecture of Mair, and describes Robin Hood as the hero of the Saxon serfs, the chief of a troop of Saxon banditti, that continued, even to the reign of Coeur de Lion, a determined resistance against the Norman invaders,[3]--and when another able and plausible writer accepts and maintains, with equal confidence, the hypothesis of Bower, and exhibits the renowned outlaw as an adherent of Simon de Montfort, who, after the fatal battle of Evesham, kept up a vigorous guerilla warfare against the officers of the tyrant Henry the Third, and of his successor,[4] we must regard these representations, which were conjectural three or four centuries ago, as conjectures still, and even as arbitrary conjectures, unless one or the other can be proved from the only _authorities_ we have, the ballads, to have a peculiar intrinsic probability.
10138-8{_}888{_}That neither of them possesses this intrinsic probability may easily be shown; but first it will be advisable to notice another theory, which is more plausibly founded on internal evidence, and claims to be confirmed by documents of unimpeachable validity.
10138-8{_}889{_}This theory has been propounded by the Rev.
10138-8{_}890{_}John Hunter, in one of his "Critical and Historical Tracts.
10138-8{_}891{_}"[5] Mr. Hunter admits that Robin Hood "lives only as a hero of song"; that he is not found in authentic contemporary chronicles; and that, when we find him mentioned in history, "the information was derived from the ballads, and is not independent of them or correlative with them."
10138-8{_}892{_}While making these admissions, he accords a considerable degree of credibility to the ballads, and particularly to the "Lytell Geste," the last two _fits_ of which he regards as giving a tolerably accurate account of real occurrences.
10138-8{_}893{_}In this part of the story King Edward is represented as coming to Nottingham to take Robin Hood.
10138-8{_}894{_}He traverses Lancashire and a part of Yorkshire, and finds his forests nearly stripped of their deer, but can get no trace of the author of these extensive depredations.
10138-8{_}895{_}At last, by the advice of one of his foresters, assuming with several of his knights the dress of a monk, he proceeds from Nottingham to Sherwood, and there soon encounters the object of his search.
10138-8{_}896{_}He submits to plunder as a matter of course, and then announces himself as a messenger sent to invite Robin Hood to the royal presence.
10138-8{_}897{_}The outlaw receives this message with great respect.
10138-8{_}898{_}There is no man in the world, he says, whom he loves so much as his king.
10138-8{_}899{_}The monk is invited to remain and dine; and after the repast an exhibition of archery is ordered, in which a bad shot is to be punished by a buffet from the hand of the chieftain.
10138-8{_}900{_}Robin, having himself once failed of the mark, requests the monk to administer the penalty.
10138-8{_}901{_}He receives a staggering blow, which rouses his suspicions, recognizes the king on an attentive consideration of his countenance, entreats grace for himself and his followers, and is freely pardoned on condition that he and they shall enter into the king's service.
10138-8{_}902{_}To this he agrees, and for fifteen months resides at court.
10138-8{_}903{_}At the end of this time he has lost all his followers but two, and spent all his money, and feels that he shall pine to death with sorrow in such a life.
10138-8{_}904{_}He returns accordingly to the greenwood, collects his old followers around him, and for twenty-two years maintains his independence in defiance of the power of Edward.
10138-8{_}905{_}Without asserting the literal verity of all the particulars of this narrative, Mr. Hunter attempts to show that it contains a substratum of fact.
10138-8{_}906{_}Edward the First, he informs us, was never in Lancashire after he became king; and if Edward the Third was ever there at all, it was not in the early years of his reign.
10138-8{_}907{_}But Edward the Second did make one single progress in Lancashire, and this in the year 1323.
10138-8{_}908{_}During this progress the king spent some time at Nottingham, and took particular note of the condition of his forests, and among these of the forest of Sherwood.
10138-8{_}909{_}Supposing now that the incidents detailed in the "Lytell Geste" really took place at this time, Robin Hood must have entered into the royal service before the end of the year 1353.
10138-8{_}910{_}It is a singular, and in the opinion of Mr. Hunter a very pregnant coincidence, that in certain Exchequer documents, containing accounts of expenses in the king's household, the name of Robyn Hode (or Robert Hood) is found several times, beginning with the 24th of March, 1324, among the "porters of the chamber" of the king.
10138-8{_}911{_}He received, with Simon Hood and others, the wages of three pence a day.
10138-8{_}912{_}In August of the following year Robin Hood suffers deduction from his pay for non-attendance, his absences grow frequent, and on the 22d of November he is discharged with a present of five shillings, "_poar cas qil ne poait pluis travailler_.
10138-8{_}913{_}"[6]  It remains still for Mr. Hunter to account for the existence of a band of seven score of outlaws in the reign of Edward the Second, in or about Yorkshire.
10138-8{_}914{_}The stormy and troublous reigns of the Plantagenets make this a matter of no difficulty.
10138-8{_}915{_}Running his finger down the long list of rebellions and commotions, he finds that early in 1322 England was convulsed by the insurrection of Thomas, Earl of Lancaster, the king's near relation, supported by many powerful noblemen.
10138-8{_}916{_}The Earl's chief seat was the castle of Pontefract, in the West Riding of Yorkshire.
10138-8{_}917{_}He is said to have been popular, and it would be a fair inference that many of his troops were raised in this part of England.
10138-8{_}918{_}King Edward easily got the better of the rebels, and took exemplary vengeance upon them.
10138-8{_}919{_}Many of the leaders were at once put to death, and the lives of all their partisans were in danger.
10138-8{_}920{_}Is it impossible, then, asks Mr. Hunter, that some who had been in the army of the Earl secreted themselves in the woods, and turned their skill in archery against the king's subjects or the king's deer?
10138-8{_}921{_}"that these were the men who for so long a time haunted Barnsdale and Sherwood, and that Robin Hood was one of them, a chief amongst them, being really of a rank originally somewhat superior to the rest?"
10138-8{_}922{_}We have, then, three different hypotheses concerning Robin Hood: one placing him in the reign of Richard the First, another in that of Henry the Third, and the last under Edward the Second, and all describing him as a political foe to the established government.
10138-8{_}923{_}To all of these hypotheses there are two very obvious and decisive objections.
10138-8{_}924{_}The first is, that Robin Hood, as already remarked, is not so much as named in contemporary history.
10138-8{_}925{_}Whether as the unsubdued leader of the Saxon peasantry, or insurgent against the tyranny of Henry or Edward, it is inconceivable that we should not hear something of him from the chroniclers.
10138-8{_}926{_}If, as Thierry says, "he had chosen Hereward for his model," it is unexplained and inexplicable why his historical fate has been so different from that of Hereward.
10138-8{_}927{_}The hero of the Camp of Refuge fills an ample place in the annals of his day; his achievements are also handed down in a prose romance, which presents many points of resemblance to the ballads of Robin Hood.
10138-8{_}928{_}It would have been no wonder, if the vulgar legends about Hereward had utterly perished; but it is altogether anomalous that a popular champion[7] who attained so extraordinary a notoriety in song, a man living from one hundred to two hundred and fifty years later than Hereward, should be passed over without one word of notice from any authoritative historian.
10138-8{_}929{_}[8] That this would not be so we are most fortunately able to demonstrate by reference to a real case which furnishes a singularly exact parallel to the present,--that of the famous outlaw, Adam Gordon.
10138-8{_}930{_}In the year 1267, says the continuator of Matthew Paris, a soldier by the name of Adam Gordon, who had lost his estates with other adherents of Simon de Montfort, and refused to seek the mercy of the king, established himself with others in like circumstances near a woody and tortuous road between the village of Wilton and the castle of Farnham, from which position he made forays into the country round about, directing his attacks especially against those who were of the king's party.
10138-8{_}931{_}Prince Edward had heard much of the prowess and honorable character of this man, and desired to have some personal knowledge of him.
10138-8{_}932{_}He succeeded in surprising Gordon with a superior force, and engaged him in single combat, forbidding any of his own followers to interfere.
10138-8{_}933{_}They fought a long time, and the prince was so filled with admiration of the courage and spirit of his antagonist, that he promised him life and fortune on condition of his surrendering.
10138-8{_}934{_}To these terms Gordon acceded, his estates were restored, and Edward found him ever after an attached and faithful servant.
10138-8{_}935{_}[9] The story is romantic, and yet Adam Gordon was not made the subject of ballads.
10138-8{_}936{_}_Caruit vate sacro_.
10138-8{_}937{_}The contemporary historians, however, all have a paragraph for him.
10138-8{_}938{_}He is celebrated by Wikes, the Chronicle of Dunstaple, the Waverley Annals, and we know not where else besides.
10138-8{_}939{_}But these theories are open to an objection stronger even than the silence of history.
10138-8{_}940{_}They are contradicted by the spirit of the ballads.
10138-8{_}941{_}No line of these songs breathes political animosity.
10138-8{_}942{_}There is no suggestion or reminiscence of wrong, from invading Norman, or from the established sovereign.
10138-8{_}943{_}On the contrary, Robin loved no man in the world so well as his king.
10138-8{_}944{_}What the tone of these ballads would have been, had Robin Hood been any sort of partisan, we may judge from the mournful and indignant strains which were poured out on the fall of De Montfort.
10138-8{_}945{_}We should have heard of the fatal field of Hastings, of the perfidy of Henry, of the sanguinary revenge of Edward,--and not of matches at archery and encounters at quarter-staff, the plundering of rich abbots and squabbles with the sheriff.
10138-8{_}946{_}The Robin Hood of our ballads is neither patriot under ban, nor proscribed rebel.
10138-8{_}947{_}An outlaw indeed he is, but an "outlaw for venyson," like Adam Bell, and one who superadds to deer-stealing the irregularity of a genteel highway-robbery.
10138-8{_}948{_}Thus much of these conjectures in general.
10138-8{_}949{_}To recur to the particular evidence by which Mr. Hunter's theory is supported, this consists principally in the name of Robin Hood being found among the king's servants shortly after Edward the Second returned from his visit to the north of his dominions.
10138-8{_}950{_}But the value of this coincidence depends entirely upon the rarity of the name.
10138-8{_}951{_}[10] Now Hood, as Mr. Hunter himself remarks, is a well-established hereditary name in the reigns of the Edwards.
10138-8{_}952{_}We find it very frequently in the indexes to the Record Publications, and this although it does not belong to the higher class of people.
10138-8{_}953{_}That Robert was an ordinary Christian name requires no proof; and if it was, the combination of Robert Hood must have been frequent also.
10138-8{_}954{_}We have taken no extraordinary pains to hunt up this combination, for really the matter is altogether too trivial to justify the expense of time; but since to some minds much may depend on the coincidence in question, we will cite several Robin Hoods in the reigns of the Edwards.
10138-8{_}955{_}28th Ed.
10138-8{_}956{_}I. Robert Hood, a citizen of London, says Mr. Hunter, supplied the king's household with beer.
10138-8{_}957{_}30th Ed.
10138-8{_}958{_}I. Robert Hood is sued for three acres of pasture land in Throckley, Northumberland.
10138-8{_}959{_}(_Rot.
10138-8{_}960{_}Orig.
10138-8{_}961{_}Abbrev._)  7th Ed.
10138-8{_}962{_}II.
10138-8{_}963{_}Robert Hood is surety for a burgess returned for Lostwithiel, Cornwall.
10138-8{_}964{_}(_Parliamentary Writs_.)
10138-8{_}965{_}9th Ed.
10138-8{_}966{_}II.
10138-8{_}967{_}Robert Hood is a citizen of Wakefield, Yorkshire, whom Mr. Hunter (p. 47) "may be justly charged with carrying supposition too far" in striving to identify with Robin the porter.
10138-8{_}968{_}10th Ed.
10138-8{_}969{_}III.
10138-8{_}970{_}A Robert Hood, of Howden, York, is mentioned in the _Calendarium Rot.
10138-8{_}971{_}Patent_.
10138-8{_}972{_}Adding the Robin Hood of the 17th Ed.
10138-8{_}973{_}II.
10138-8{_}974{_}we have six persons of that name mentioned within a period of less than forty years, and this circumstance does not dispose us to receive with great favor any argument that may be founded upon one individual case of its occurrence.
10138-8{_}975{_}But there is no end to the absurdities which flow from this supposition.
10138-8{_}976{_}We are to believe that the weak and timid prince, that had severely punished his kinsman and his nobles, freely pardoned a yeoman, who, after serving with the rebels, had for twenty months made free with the king's deer and robbed on the highway,--and not only pardoned him, but received him into service _near his person_.
10138-8{_}977{_}We are further to believe that the man who had led so daring and jovial a life, and had so generously dispensed the pillage of opulent monks, willingly entered into this service, doffed his Lincoln green for the Plantagenet plush, and _consented_ to be enrolled among royal flunkies for three pence a day.
10138-8{_}978{_}And again, admitting all this, we are finally obliged by Mr. Hunter's document to concede that the stalworth archer (who, according to the ballad, maintained himself two-and-twenty years in the wood) was worn out by his duties as "proud porter" in less than two years, and was discharged a superannuated lackey, with five shillings in his pocket, _"poar cas qil ne poait pluis travailler"!_  To those who are well acquainted with ancient popular poetry the adventure of King Edward and Robin Hood will seem the least eligible portion of this circle of story for the foundation of an historical theory.
10138-8{_}979{_}The ballad of King Edward and Robin Hood is but one version of an extremely multiform legend, of which the tales of "King Edward and the Shepherd" and "King Edward and the Hermit" are other specimens; and any one who will take the trouble to examine will be convinced that all these stories are one and the same thing, the personages being varied for the sake of novelty, and the name of a recent or of the reigning monarch substituted in successive ages for that of a predecessor.
10138-8{_}980{_}Rejecting, then, as nugatory, every attempt to assign Robin Hood a definite position in history, what view shall we adopt?
10138-8{_}981{_}Are all these traditions absolute fictions, and is he himself a pure creation of the imagination?
10138-8{_}982{_}Might not the ballads under consideration have a basis in the exploits of a real person, living in the forests, _somewhere_ and _at some time?_ Or, denying individual existence to Robin Hood, and particular truth to the adventures ascribed to him, may we not regard him as the ideal of the outlaw class, a class so numerous in all the countries of Europe in the Middle Ages?
10138-8{_}983{_}We are perfectly contented to form no opinion upon the subject; but if compelled to express one, we should say that this last supposition (which is no novelty) possessed decidedly more likelihood than any other.
10138-8{_}984{_}Its plausibility will be confirmed by attending to the apparent signification of the name Robin Hood.
10138-8{_}985{_}The natural refuge and stronghold of the outlaw was the woods.
10138-8{_}986{_}Hence he is termed by Latin writers _silvatious,_ by the Normans _forestier_.
10138-8{_}987{_}The Anglo-Saxon robber or highwayman is called a woodrover _wealdgenga,_ and the Norse word for outlaw is exactly equivalent.
10138-8{_}988{_}[11] It has often been suggested that Robin Hood is a corruption, or dialectic form, of Robin of the Wood; and when we remember that _wood_ is pronounced _hood_ in some parts of England,[12] (as _whoop_ is pronounced _hoop_ everywhere,) and that the outlaw bears in so many languages a name descriptive of his habitation, this notion will not seem an idle fancy.
10138-8{_}989{_}Various circumstances, however, have disposed writers of learning to look farther for a solution of the question before us.
10138-8{_}990{_}Mr. Wright propounds an hypothesis that Robin Hood "one among the personages of the early mythology of the Teutonic peoples"; and a German scholar,[13] in an exceedingly interesting article which throws much light on the history of English sports, has endeavored to show specifically that he is in name and substance one with the god Woden.
10138-8{_}991{_}The arguments by which these views are supported, though in their present shape very far from convincing, are entitled to a respectful consideration.
10138-8{_}992{_}The most important of these arguments are those which are based on the peculiar connection between Robin Hood and the month of May.
10138-8{_}993{_}Mr. Wright has justly remarked, that either an express mention of this month, or a vivid description of the season, in the older ballads, shows that the feats of the hero were generally performed during this part of the year.
10138-8{_}994{_}Thus, the adventure of "Robin Hood and the Monk" befell on "a morning of May."
10138-8{_}995{_}"Robin Hood and the Potter" and "Robin Hood and Guy of Gisborne" begin, like "Robin Hood and the Monk," with a description of the season when leaves are long, blossoms are shooting, and the small birds are singing; and this season, though called summer, is at the same time spoken of as May in "Robin Hood and the Monk," which, from the description there given, it needs must be.
10138-8{_}996{_}The liberation of Cloudesly by Adam Bel and Clym of the Clough is also achieved "on a merry morning of May."
10138-8{_}997{_}Robin Hood is, moreover, intimately associated with the month of May through the games which were celebrated at that time of the year.
10138-8{_}998{_}The history of these games is unfortunately very defective, and hardly extends farther back than the beginning of the sixteenth century.
10138-8{_}999{_}By that time their primitive character seems to have been corrupted, or at least their significance was so far forgotten, that distinct pastimes and ceremonials were capriciously intermixed.
10138-8{_}1000{_}At the beginning of the sixteenth century the May sports in vogue were, besides a contest of archery, four _pageants_,--the Kingham, or election of a Lord and Lady of the May, otherwise called Summer King and Queen, the Morris-Dance, the Hobby-Horse, and the "Robin Hood."
10138-8{_}1001{_}Though these pageants were diverse in their origin, they had, at the epoch of which we write, begun to be confounded; and the Morris exhibited a tendency to absorb and blend them all, as, from its character, being a procession interspersed with dancing, it easily might do.
10138-8{_}1002{_}We shall hardly find the Morris pure and simple in the English May-game; but from a comparison of the two earliest representations which we have of this sport, the Flemish print given by Douce in his "Illustrations of Shakspeare," and Tollett's celebrated painted window, (described in Johnson and Steevens's Shakspeare,) we may form an idea of what was essential and what adventitious in the English spectacle.
10138-8{_}1003{_}The Lady is evidently the central personage in both.
10138-8{_}1004{_}She is, we presume, the same as the Queen of May, who is the oldest of all the characters in the May games, and the apparent successor to the Goddess of Spring in the Roman Floralia.
10138-8{_}1005{_}In the English Morris she is called simply The Lady, or more frequently Maid Marian, a name which, to our apprehension, means Lady of the May, and nothing more.
10138-8{_}1006{_}[14] A fool and a taborer seem also to have been indispensable; but the other dancers had neither names nor peculiar offices, and were unlimited in number.
10138-8{_}1007{_}The Morris, then, though it lost in allegorical significance, would gain considerably in spirit and variety by combining with the other shows.
10138-8{_}1008{_}Was it not natural, therefore, and in fact inevitable, that the old favorites of the populace, Robin Hood, Friar Tuck, and Little John, should in the course of time displace three of the anonymous performers in the show?
10138-8{_}1009{_}This they had pretty effectually done at the beginning of the sixteenth century; and the Lady, who had accepted the more precise designation of Maid Marian, was after that generally regarded as the consort of Robin Hood, though she sometimes appeared in the Morris without him.
10138-8{_}1010{_}In like manner, the Hobby-Horse was quite early adopted into the Morris, of which it formed no original part, and at last even a Dragon was annexed to the company.
10138-8{_}1011{_}Under these circumstances we cannot be surprised to find the principal performers in the May pageants passing the one into the other,--to find the May King, whose occupation was gone when the gallant outlaw had supplanted him in the favor of the Lady, assuming the part of the Hobby-Horse,[15] Robin Hood usurping the title of King of the May,[16] and the Hobby-Horse entering into a contest with the Dragon, as St. George.
10138-8{_}1012{_}We feel obliged to regard this interchange of functions among the characters in the English May-pageants as fortuitous, notwithstanding the coincidence of the May King sometimes appearing on horseback in Germany, and notwithstanding our conviction that Kuhn is right in maintaining that the May King, the Hobby-Horse, and the Dragon-Slayer are symbols of one mythical idea.
10138-8{_}1013{_}This idea we are compelled by want of space barely to state, with the certainty of doing injustice to the learning and ingenuity with which the author has supported his views.
10138-8{_}1014{_}Kuhn has shown it to be extremely probable, first, that the Christmas games, which both in Germany and England have a close resemblance to those of Spring, are to be considered as a prelude to the May sports, and that they both originally symbolized the victory of Summer over Winter,[17] which, beginning at the winter solstice, is completed in the second month of spring; secondly, that the conquering Summer is represented by the May King, or by the Hobby-Horse (as also by the Dragon-Slayer, whether St. George, Siegfried, Apollo, or the Sanskrit Indras); and thirdly, that the Hobby-Horse in particular represents the god Woden, who, as well as Mars [18] among the Romans, is the god at once of Spring and of Victory.
10138-8{_}1015{_}The essential point, all this being admitted, is now to establish the identity of Robin Hood and the Hobby-Horse.
10138-8{_}1016{_}This we think we have shown cannot be done by reasoning founded on the early history of the games under consideration.
10138-8{_}1017{_}Kuhn relies principally upon two modern accounts of Christmas pageants.
10138-8{_}1018{_}In one of these pageants there is introduced a man on horseback, who carries in his hands a bow and arrows.
10138-8{_}1019{_}The other furnishes nothing peculiar except a name: the ceremony is called a _hoodening,_ and the hobby-horse a _hooden_.
10138-8{_}1020{_}In the rider with bow and arrows Kuhn sees Robin Hood and the Hobby-Horse, and in the name _hooden_ (which is explained by the authority he quotes to mean wooden) he discovers a provincial form of wooden, which connects the outlaw and the divinity.
10138-8{_}1021{_}[19] It will be generally agreed that these slender premises are totally inadequate to support the weighty conclusion that is rested upon them.
10138-8{_}1022{_}Why the adventures of Robin Hood should be specially assigned, as they are in the old ballads, to the month of May, remains unexplained.
10138-8{_}1023{_}We have no exquisite reason to offer, but we may perhaps find reason good enough in the delicious stanzas with which some of these ballads begin.
10138-8{_}1024{_}"In summer when the shawÃ¨s be sheen,        And leavÃ¨s be large and long,      It is full merry in fair forÃ¨st        To hear the fowlÃ¨s song;      To see the deer draw to the dale,        And leave the hillÃ¨s hee,      And shadow them in the leavÃ¨s green        Under the green-wood tree."
10138-8{_}1025{_}The poetical character of the season affords all the explanation that is required.
10138-8{_}1026{_}Nor need the occurrence of exhibitions of archery and of the Robin Hood plays and pageants, at this time of the year, occasion any difficulty.
10138-8{_}1027{_}Repeated statutes, from the thirteenth to the sixteenth century, enjoined practice with the bow, and ordered that the leisure time of holidays should be employed for this purpose.
10138-8{_}1028{_}Under Henry the Eighth the custom was still kept up, and those who partook in this exercise often gave it a spirit by assuming the style and character of Robin Hood and his associates.
10138-8{_}1029{_}In like manner the society of archers in Elizabeth's time took the name of Arthur and his Knights; all which was very natural then, and would be now.
10138-8{_}1030{_}None of all the merrymakings in merry England surpassed the May festival.
10138-8{_}1031{_}The return of the sun stimulated the populace to the accumulation of all sorts of amusements.
10138-8{_}1032{_}In addition to the traditional and appropriate sports of the season, there were, as Stowe tells us, divers warlike shows, with good archers, morris-dancers, and other devices for pastime all day long, and towards evening stage-plays and bonfires in the streets.
10138-8{_}1033{_}A Play of Robin Hood was considered "very proper for a May-game"; but if Robin Hood was peculiarly prominent in these entertainments, the obvious reason would appear to be that he was the hero of that loved green-wood to which all the world resorted, when the cold obstruction of winter was broken up, "to do observance for a morn of May."
10138-8{_}1034{_}We do not, therefore, attribute much value to the theory of Mr. Wright, that the May festival was, in its earliest form, "a religious celebration, though, like such festivals in general, it possessed a double character, that of a religious ceremony, and of an opportunity for the performance of warlike games; that, at such festivals, the songs would take the character of the amusements on the occasion, and would most likely celebrate warlike deeds,--perhaps the myths of the patron whom superstition supposed to preside over them; that, as the character of the exercises changed, the attributes of the patron would change also, and he who was once celebrated as working wonders with his good axe or his elf-made sword might afterwards assume the character of a skilful bowman; that the scene of his actions would likewise change, and the person whose weapons were the bane of dragons and giants, who sought them in the wildernesses they infested, might become the enemy only of the sheriff and his officers, under the 'grene-wode lefe.'"
10138-8{_}1035{_}It is unnecessary to point out that the language we have quoted contains, beyond the statement that warlike exercises were anciently combined with religious rites, a very slightly founded surmise, and nothing more.
10138-8{_}1036{_}Another circumstance, which weighs much with Mr. Wright, goes but a very little way with us in demonstrating the mythological character of Robin Hood.
10138-8{_}1037{_}This is the frequency with which his name is attached to mounds, wells, and stones, such as in the popular creed are connected with fairies, dwarfs, or giants.
10138-8{_}1038{_}There is scarcely a county in England which does not possess some monument of this description.
10138-8{_}1039{_}"Cairns on Blackdown in Somersetshire, and barrows near to Whitby in Yorkshire and Ludlow in Shropshire, are termed Robin Hood's pricks or butts; lofty natural eminences in Gloucestershire and Derbyshire are Robin Hood's hills; a huge rock near Matlock is Robin Hood's Tor; ancient boundary-stones, as in Lincolnshire, are Robin Hood's crosses; a presumed loggan, or rocking-stone, in Yorkshire, is Robin Hood's penny-stone; a fountain near Nottingham, another between Doncaster and Wakefield, and one in Lancashire, are Robin Hood's wells; a cave in Nottinghamshire is his stable; a rude natural rock in Hope Dale is his chair; a chasm at Chatsworth is his leap; Blackstone Edge, in Lancashire, is his bed.
10138-8{_}1040{_}"[20] In fact, his name bids fair to overrun every remarkable object of the sort which has not been already appropriated to King Arthur or the Devil; with the latter of whom, at least, it is presumed, that, however ancient, he will not dispute precedence.
10138-8{_}1041{_}"The legends of the peasantry," quoth Mr. Wright, "are the shadows of a very remote antiquity."
10138-8{_}1042{_}This proposition, thus broadly stated, we deny.
10138-8{_}1043{_}Nothing is more deceptive than popular legends; and the "legends" we speak of, if they are to bear that name, have no claim to antiquity at all.
10138-8{_}1044{_}They do not go beyond the ballads.
10138-8{_}1045{_}They are palpably of subsequent and comparatively recent origin.
10138-8{_}1046{_}It was absolutely impossible that they should arise while Robin Hood was a living reality to the people.
10138-8{_}1047{_}The archer of Sherwood who could barely stand King Edward's buffet, and was felled by the Potter, was no man to be playing with rocking-stones.
10138-8{_}1048{_}This trick of naming must have begun in the decline of his fame; for there was a time when his popularity drooped, and his existence was just not doubted,--not elaborately maintained by learned historians, and antiquarians deeply read in the Public Records.
10138-8{_}1049{_}And what do these names prove?
10138-8{_}1050{_}The vulgar passion for bestowing them is notorious and universal.
10138-8{_}1051{_}We Americans are too young to be well provided with heroes that might serve this purpose.
10138-8{_}1052{_}We have no imaginative peasantry to invent legends, no ignorant peasantry to believe them.
10138-8{_}1053{_}But we have the good fortune to possess the Devil in common with the rest of the world; and we take it upon us to say, that there is not a mountain district in the land, which has been opened to summer travellers, where a "Devil's Bridge," a "Devil's Punch-bowl," or some object with the like designation, will not be pointed out.
10138-8{_}1054{_}[21]  We have taken no notice of the later fortunes of Robin Hood in his true and original character of a hero of romance.
10138-8{_}1055{_}Towards the end of the sixteenth century Anthony Munday attempted to revive the decaying popularity of this king of good fellows, who had won all his honors as a simple yeoman, by representing him in the play of "The Downfall of Robert, Earl of Huntington" as a nobleman in disguise, outlawed by the machinations of his steward.
10138-8{_}1056{_}This pleasing and successful drama is Robin's sole patent to that title of Earl of Huntington, in confirmation of which Dr. Stukeley fabricated a pedigree that transcends even the absurdities of heraldry, and some unknown forger an epitaph beneath the skill of a Chatterton.
10138-8{_}1057{_}Those who desire a full acquaintance with the fabulous history of Robin Hood will seek it in the well-known volumes of Ritson, or in those of his recent editor, Gutch, who does not make up by superior discrimination for his inferiority in other respects to that industrious antiquary.
